RELEVANT PUBLICATIONS, PRODUCTS, SERVICES, OR OTHER ACHIEVEMENTS 
 
EFS Financial Suite (EFS). The EFS Financial Suite is based on the outcomes of previous innovative research work 
conveyed by EXUS in the framework of EC co-funded projects. In specific, EFS uses best of breed technologies in 
the field of scalable data analytics, pattern recognition and analysis, machine learning for continuous adaptation and 
state of the art visualisation techniques. EFS provides the necessary tools for supporting all the steps of the Risk 
scoring and management, Loan Origination, Debt Collection and Recovery process for financial institutions or other 
organisations that deal with transaction -heavy processes in real -time applications (e.g. Telcos, Brokers, Insurance 
Companies etc.). EFS product enables non -IT expert users (collection managers) to develop detailed coll ection 
strategies and to define rules for implementing the strategies, visually through intuitive web tools. So based on the 
requirements of each customer, every step of the strategy can be set -up using the most suitable paradigm. The 
representation is tra nslated to specific business processes that are understandable and editable by the collection 
managers. The EFS enables on the fly changes, as well as the automated production of self-adapting strategies taking 
into account the whole cycle of the collectio n process from pre -collections (e.g. SMS notifications for high risk 
customers before the delinquency date) to early, restructuring, pre -legal, legal, write off and portfolio sale. To that 
effect, the EFS core engine aggregates disparate data sources both internal and external. Therefore, large historic data 
sets within the organisation are fully exploited for pattern identification and extraction while social media and other 
real-time consumer behavioural attributes are leveraged to yield a complete pictur e. EXUS is an expert in enabling 
intelligent action in enterprise systems through effective and value -adding real-time information management. In 
2016, EFS was ranked “Best in Class in the world” from the CEB Tower group. http://collections.exus.co.uk/loan-
collections-systems-technology-analysis 
 
Web and Mobile Apps. EXUS designs and develops sophisticated Web-based and Mobile (native/cross-platform) 
Apps in various d omains ranging from Banking and Telecom Markets (commercial products) to crowd safety and 
first responders, health and assisting living arenas (Research activities). These include running transaction -heavy 
intranets and extranets and e-learning, e-payment and iOS and Android phone apps. Indicative clients are Vodafone, 
WIND, Greek Public Power Supply Company, Historic Hotels of Europe, etc. By exploiting our rich experience in 
delivering complex and large scale Internet technology projects and our exposure to state -of-the-art research 
outcomes, we are currently introducing to the market an end-to-end solution covering the needs of fast and effective 
digital presence for all types of organisations. To that effect, our newly delivered platforms remove the comp lexity 
from the traditional approaches in building information -heavy portals and applications, to the benefit of the end -
user/customer. It is a fully cloud-deployable solution going under the PaaS model. It empowers non-technical people 
to create, design, parameterize, launch, market and manage all types of native applications exploiting smart-phones’ 
potential to the fullest. Its next release planned for Q4 2014, will entail a series of analytics functions in order to 
leverage the ability to monitor consumer/user behaviour for the delivery of increased value to EXUS’ customers.  
 
EXUS Analytical Framework. (EAF) . EXUS Analytics Framework Analytics Framework is built upon a 
distributed, multi-node Hadoop cluster backed by the Hadoop Distributed File System (HDFS), running on Ubuntu 
Linux 14.04 LTS. More precisely, the Cloudera Distribution Hadoop (CDH) cluster that resides on Azure cloud 
platform is deployed. Cloudera provides a data -management platform designed specifically to address the 
opportunities and challenges of big data. By uniting flexible storage and processing under a single management 
framework and set of system resources, Cloudera delivers the versatility and agility required for modern data 
management. EXUS Analytics Framework is a gene ric analytics platform that can be adapted to the needs of the 
scenario under investigation and the data provided to the platform. The driving idea behind the design and 
development of EAF is the seamless facilitation of analytics functions for both batch and streaming data (including 
“Big Data”) by different typed of business users, without imposing strong requirements on expertise and 
specialisation. To achieve that, EAF employs a codeless approach in the sense that the end -to-end platform is 
integrated in such a way, so that the end users can interact with it, without requiring extra effort.  
EXus Artificial Intelligence TEchnologies EXAITE Suite.   
Section 4,5,6-page 75 
 
 
 
One of the key areas of focus within EXUS is predictive modelling and its application to a wide variety of fields. 
EXAITE Suite consists of computational intelligence tools to support the lifecycle of any potential artificial 
intelligence project. Exploratory data analysis techniques can be used to explore high-dimensional data with the goal 
of discovering s tructure or patterns that lend to the formation of statistical hypotheses, visualizing the data and 
analysing them. Depending on whether unstructured or structured transactional and relational datasets occur, deep 
learning approaches or deep feature synthe sis approaches are available in the context of an automated feature 
engineering. In addition to this, feature extraction techniques (e.g., Principal Component Analysis, Factor Analysis, 
Isometric Feature Mapping, Auto -encoders), as well as filter -based (e.g., Mutual Information) and wrapper -based 
feature selection techniques (e.g., Genetic Algorithm) are available to rank features and discover knowledge from 
raw data by selecting the most appropriate subset of features in a dimensionality reduction context.  Cutting edge 
artificial intelligence algorithms based on deep learning, extreme learning machine, ensemble tree -based models, 
evolutionary optimization and swarm intelligence are provided for prediction and forecasting purposes (e.g., time -
series machine learning). Clustering algorithms (e.g., weighted clustering, spectral clustering), bayesian networks, 
anomaly detection algorithms and fuzzy-rule based inference are available to design a decision support process for 
rational decision making and/or a risk assessment policy (e.g., early warning system) for threat/attack detection or 
fault detection purposes. EXUS has developed an internal library of AI tools that can be re-used across various fields. 
More specifically the suite has the following tools: 1) Predictive model for patient readmission prediction which has 
shown excellent results in field tests. 2) Predictive Model for fraud detection that provide risk estimates for online 
transactions in real time. 3) Predictive models for boat engine fault prediction 4) crop failure prediction for insurance 
risk analysis. 5) EXUS has applied state-of-the-art methods to develop recommenders that allow collection processes, 
in financial institutions, to be tailored to the customer. This is achieved by analysing response patterns of customers 
to collection actions, combining with freely available social media data and developing targeted models for 
recommending actions best suited to the customer. 6) Another broad area of focus of analytics work undertaken by 
EXUS revolves around the use of natural language processing (NLP). EXUS is actively engaged in developing AI 
powered chatbots and sentiment analysis to support customer interactions for its clients. Works undertaken focus on 
applying these advanced techniques to ut ilize textual and voice data retained by its clients to feed into predictive 
models that deliver actionable insights. EXUS has also undertaken several projects delivering visual analytics 
solutions in the Healthcare and Financial verticals. Solutions developed range from customizable dashboards to drive 
business intelligence (BI) to advanced analytics visualizations tailored for the customer data. EXUS is exploit and 
transfer this AI expertise in the security sector, developing predictive models, simulated scenarios and smart 
algorithms to facilitate the crisis operators and the first responders.

4.1.3 7 SOLP -SOLPLAST, SA  
 
PARTNER Nº: 37 (Large industry)  
WEBSITE: www.solplast.com 
SHORTNAME: SOLP  
LOCATION: Spain 
ROLE IN THE PROJECT: PARTNER.  
 
DESCRIPTION OF THE LEGAL ENTITY  
 
SOLPLAST is a strong national leader specialized in agricultural plastics, with the highest production figures in 
Spain, and a major global competitor, exporting to more than 40 countries worldwide.  SOLPLAST belongs to the 
Armando Álvarez business group, which is currently the largest Spanish processor of polyethylene plastic films 
with an annual volume of 300,000 tonnes.  
The Group consists of 15 companies, call complementing one another, specializing in different segments and 
applications in the plastics market, enabling us to have a global vision of the sector, integrate a variety of solutions 
and serve as a launch pad for new technological developments. The Armando Álvarez Group is present with its 
own offices in Germany, Benelux, France, United Kingdom, Portugal, United States, Mexico, Morocco and 
Turkey. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Some of the SOLPLAST most significant worldwide clients are: 
• AGROQUÍMICOS LOS TRIVIÑOS 
• BONNYSA 
• INTEGRAL S XXI 
• SUREXPORT 
• FRUTAS MARIPI 
• SAT BIONEST 

Section 4,5,6-page 79 
 
• UNION INVIVO 
• GOLDCROP 
• BEISELEN GMBH  
SOLPLAST has an annual production capacity of 60,000 metric tons, using multilayer coextrusion technology. 
In manufacturing their films, SOLPLAST combine low -density polyethylene (LDPE), linear low-density 
polyethylene (LLDPE), metallocenes and ethylene-vinyl acetate (EVA)/butyl acrylate (EBA) copolymer, along with 
other types of specific, more technical polymers. SOLPLAST can extrude films from 10 microns (40 gauge) and up 
to 1 mm (4,000 gauge), and with widths up to 20 m. SOLPLAST adapts its production to its clients’ requirements in 
terms of dimensions , mechanical & barrier characteristics  and different bobbin presentations. 
Its knowledge of different markets, service capacity and logistic development means they can offer the most advanced 
solutions in agricultural plastics and continue contributing to more sustainable and profitable crops. 
 
SOLPLAST applies active R&D&I policies, testing new applications with polymers and additives in conjuncti on 
with its suppliers, universities and research institutes. They also work shoulder-to-shoulder with their clients to adapt 
their developments to their specific applications. SOLPLAST constantly invests in new technologies and create new 
products to solve highly specific problems based on each crop and field of application. SOLPLAST works actively 
to protect the environment by applying techniques that minimize production emissions and waste while conducting 
research into reducing the environmental impact of all its products. 
 
MAIN TASKS IN THE PROJECT 
 
SOLP will be involved in WP1 collaborating in the definition of the A2C agricultural plastics requirements (T1.1). 
In WP3 they will collaborate in the developments of processable recycled plastics compounds (T3.5). In WP5 they 
will be involved in the plastics compounds transformation into agricultural films at small scale (T5.4). In WP6 they 
will be end users by producing ag ricultural mulch films and disinfection films at relevant and industrial relevant 
environment to demonstrate our solution at TRL6-7. 
 
Related experience: SOLP is one of the key European players in the plastics transformation into agricultural films, 
having a vast experience in cast and blown extrusion. Moreover, they are committed to the innovation by a R&D 
structure focus on the development of its own products, new systems and materials. 
 
INTEREST IN THE PROJECT 
 
SOLPLAST has a leading position in the international agricultural market . Since its creation, it has tried to 
maintain a level of quality and service to customers, which results in a continuous growth and development, both in 
the national and international market. The development of a high barrier agriculture films as alternat ive to current 
multilayers as well as biodegradable mulch films produced will give a competitive market advantage. Moreover, they 
will increase their knowledge in recyclable and biodegradable compounds. Increasing their European networking.

SOLPLAST route map for innovation involves field work to establish the real needs of farmers, the crop development 
and agricultural practices. They cover all market needs from beginning to end with the broadest, most specialised 
universe of agricultural plastics. 
Section 4,5,6-page 80 
 
▪ Greenhouse covering: 
 INDASOL: Films manufactured by multilayer coextrusion, using combinations of homopolymers (LDPE 
and LLDPE), copolymers (EVA and EBA) and metallocen e resins . Excellent mechanical properties: 
combining the flexibility of the EBA/EVA copolymers in the central layers with the rigidity and mechanical 
strength of the LLDPE materials and the metallocenes in the outer layers. The INDASOL films are thermal 
(they reduce cooling inside the greenhouse at night) and are available in different thicknesses and presentations, 
depending on the end application. Highly efficient heat-insulation, making the INDASOL films an ideal solution 
for geographical areas with sharp nighttime temperature drops. 
➢ INDASOL White 
➢ INDASOL Yellow  
➢ INDASOL White Plus 
➢  INDASOL Yellow Plus  
➢ INDASOL Q4tro 
➢  INDASOL Cristal 
➢  INDASOL TRITERMIC  
➢ INDASOL SOLAIR 
 
 DURASOL: Films manufactured by multilayer coextrusion, using homopolymers (LDPE and LLDPE), 
copolymers (EVA and EBA) and metallocene resins, combined to maximise their properties . These 
materials are non-thermal and are available in different thicknesses and presentations, depending on the end 
application. Good mechanical properties, prov iding excellent performance against damage due to weather 
conditions and handling in application. The DURAPLAST versions (White and Yellow) are specially designed 
to resist chemical degradation caused by treatments used inside greenhouses, withstanding an S content of up to 
3,000 ppm and a Cl content of up to 200 ppm. The DURASOL Antithermal and Shadow products are specifically 
designed for high-radiation areas, when the crop requires a high temperature peaks inside the greenhouse to 
be lowered. 
➢ DURASOL White  
➢ DURASOL Yellow  
➢ DURASOL Shadow  
➢ DURASOL Antithermal  
➢ DURAPLAST White  
➢ DURAPLAST Yellow 
 
 DURATERMIC: Films manufactured by multilayer coextrusion, using combinations of homopolymers 
(LDPE and LLDPE), metallocene resins and EVA/EBA copolymers . High light diffusion. This prevents 
shadows forming in the greenhouse, thus producing greater uniformity of radiation and preventing irregular crop 
growth. Excellent thermal effect, which reduces temperature drops inside the greenhouse when there is no 
external energy supply, thus reducing the risk of frost. Due to its heat-insulating efficiency, DURATERMIC 
film is specially indicated for geographical areas that produce sharp drops in temperature at night, although 
it is also suitable for milder climates.  There are different varieties within the DURATERMIC range specially 
designed for installing in macro-tunnels for growing strawberries and raspberries. 
➢ DURATERMIC White 
➢ DURATERMIC Yellow  
➢ DURATERMIC Strawberry 
➢ DURATERMIC Raspberry  
➢ DURATERMIC Antivirus 
➢ DURATERMIC Antithermal  
➢ DURATERMIC Grape 
▪ Mulch: 
 The SOLPLAST mulch products are manufactured in multilayer structures, with a combination of LDPE, 
LLDPE and metallocene resins. They are manufactured in different thicknesses and presentations, dep ending 
on the end application, but always maintaining high mechanical and agronomic qualities. Relevant for the 
present project is that SOLPLAST already commercializes a biodegradable PLA mulch film. 
➢ ACOSOL Nt NATURAL MULCH 
➢ ACOSOL Ng BLACK MULCH

Section 4,5,6-page 81 
 
➢ ACOSOL OD-Ng BLACK OXO-BIODEGRADABLE MULCH 
➢  ACOSOL Bd BLACK BIODEGRADABLE MULCH 
 
▪ Disinfection:  
 SOLPLAST soil disinfection films are manufactured in multilayer structures, with a combination of LDPE, 
LLDPE and metallocene and/or barrier resins, as well as the necessary additives. They are manufactured 
in thicknesses ranging from 20 to 50 micras, maintaining high mechanical and  agronomic qualities. Special 
high gas-barrier resins are used to reduce gas loss through the plastic (DESINFESOL Special DS) or practically 
impede such loss (DESINFESOL Barrier). Relevant for the present project is that SOLPLAS T already 
commercializes a multilayer disinfection film with until 7 layers-extrusion multilayer technology. 
▪ Double screen: 
 Double screen films are produced using resins which considerably improve light transmission into the 
greenhouse, minimising losses in the area of cultivation.  In addition, they have a thermal effect that 
strengthens that of the greenhouse roof. The SOLPLAST double screen films are manufactured at thicknesses 
starting from 37.5 micras (150 gauges). They are produced using EVA/EBA copolymers and/ or metallocenes, 
in multilayer structures, which considerably improve light transmission into the greenhouse, minimising losses 
in the area of cultivation. 
 
▪ Small tunnel: 
 Small tunnels are light, simple structures for protecting crops from the weather and external agents. Their 
small size produces very low thermal inertia, heating rapidly during the day and releasing heat rapidly at 
night. For crops or geographical areas that do not require thermal properties, SOLPLAST offers th e products 
TUNESOL Natural and TUNESOL Natural UV . These films have excellent mechanical properties, providing 
the crops with physical protection from external harm. The ultraviolet stabilization provided by TUNESOL UV 
gives it a longer life if required by the application. When a more technical product is required for this application, 
SOLPLAST offers its TUNESOL Thermal Diffuse and TUNESOL Thermal Cristal films. The composition of 
these materials includes resins with thermal properties, as well as a packag e of IR additives that improve heat 
retention, avoiding sharp drops in temperature, especially during periods of frosts. 
➢ TUNESOL Natural  
➢ TUNESOL Natural UV  
➢ TUNESOL Thermal Diffuse  
➢ TUNESOL Thermal Cristal 
 
▪ Silage 
 Silage is a conservation technique for moist grass and grain using plastic covers . It involves spontaneous 
lactic fermentation in anaerobic conditions (lack of O2). This fermentation produces acidification (pH 
reduction to around 4.2), inhibiting the activity of micro -organisms, which can degrade the product, and 
fungal activity. This process enables feed to be stored after harvesting, maintaining its nutritional quality and 
strength, thus increasing the animal count per hectare and replacing or complementing feed concentrates. 
SOLPLAST silage films are manufactured in multilayer structures, with a combination of LDPE, LLDPE and 
metallocene resins. 
➢ SILASOL Ne  
➢ SILASOL Ne+  
➢ SILASOL BN  
➢ SILASOL BN+  
➢ SILASOL Ve-Ne SILASOL  
➢ Ve-Ne+ SILASOL B 
➢  SILASOL Gr-Ne  
➢ SILASOL Gr-Ne+ 
▪ Reservoirs 
 These products for waterproofing are manufactured in thicknesses from 250 µ to 1 mm , for the 
GEOEMBALSE Standard and 500 µ for the GEOEMBALSE Welded. The GEOEMBALSE Standard is 
manufactured with a combination for LDPE and LLDPE resins, which giv e the end product high mechanical 
strength, especially against penetration and impact, making the product particularly suitable for this application. 
The GEOEMBALSE Weldable is manufactured with a three -layer structure, using LDPE, LLDPE and other 
specific material to optimize product performance in the application. 
▪ Special products 
 This group includes films manufactured for construction, protection for boats or large machinery, product 
storage and packaging, pallet fumigation, protection from external agents and more. In all cases, these films 
are characterized by their easy installation and handling and their flexibility for use in different applications. 
Section 4,5,6-page 82 
 
SOLPLAST adapts these types of products to each specific application, modifying their properties to ob tain the 
desired results in each case. 
➢ B/W FILM FOR HYDROPONIC CULTIVATION: Two-tone (black/white) sheet manufactured using LDPE, 
LLDPE and metallocenes, in thicknesses starting at 100 µ (400 gauges). It is used as a medium for hydroponic 
cultivation substrates (without soil) 
➢  STRETCHABLE CUCUMBER FILM: This product is designed specifically for packaging cucumber. Its 
mechanical and optical properties (high gloss and transparency) and its capacity to stretch make it particularly 
suitable for this application.  
➢ PROTECTION FILM: This group includes films manufactured for construction, protection for boats or large 
machinery, product storage and packaging, pallet fumigation, protection from external agents and more. 
➢  BULK CONTAINER LINER: Liners are used throughout the world to sent raw materials that require moisture 
control, odour protection and good protection against contamination. They are also used as a physical 
protection barrier to avoid contamination of the container carrying the product, thus permitting very different 
types of product to be loaded, using the same container for successive journeys. Container and flex tank liners 
are frequently used as an economical solution for the bulk transport of chemical products, grains, cereals, 
liquids, granulated products and more. The materials are designed in multilayer structures, using combinations 
of metallocene resins and EVA/EBA copolymers, which strengthen the mechanical properties of the product. 
This prevents problems of tearing due to the pressure exert ed by the load inside the liner, as well as possible 
damage caused by movement of the load during the journey.

4.1. 40 TCA -TECNOALIMENTI S.C.P.A  
 
PARTNER Nº: 40 (NPO) 
WEBSITE: www.tecnoalimenti.com 
SHORTNAME: TCA 
LOCATION: Spain 
ROLE IN THE PROJECT: PARTNER. 
 
DESCRIPTION OF THE LEGAL ENTITY  
Established in 1981 by the Italian Research Ministry for promoting, co -ordinating and carrying out research for 
the agri-food industry, Tecnoalimenti is as a non -profit research consortium of 30 food sector industries  and 
one financial institution, Intesa San Paolo, as trustee of ministerial funds. Its member industries account for about 
12% of Italian food sales.  
In almost 40 years of activity, the organisation has acquired high-level skills in: (i) industrial research, carrying out 
food chain studies, technological feasibility assessments, eco -compatibility and cost -benefits analyses of new 
processes, (ii) research project set-up and management, (iii) dissemination activities to industries and SMEs at 
national and international level through its wide industrial network.  
 
Tecnoalimenti has a wide experience in networking industries (large and SMEs) for research activities, due to its 
institutional function of bridging industry and research players. Further, Tecnoalime nti participated/participates to 
food chain projects with a specific role of carrying out studies, training activities, steering and animating Industrial 
Platforms, thanks to its wide experience in approaching food SMEs.  
 
Tecnoalimenti is in the steering board of two clusters, the Italian Agrifood Clusters at national level (CLAN) and 
the Lombardy High Tech Cluster for Agrofood CATAL that set up in the last decade. TCA is also an active member 
of and other national Platforms, such as Security Research in I taly SERIT and M2Q (Public -private laboratory for 
R&D in the agro-industrial field). This facilitates the networking and the identification of new value chains.  
 
MAIN TASKS IN THE PROJECT   
Tecnoalimenti will be involved in WP7, where they will lead the replication task (T7.6) aimed at demonstrate the 
A2C replication and scalability potential through preparing a specific replication plan based on the lessons learned, 
making it ready at projec t end the future implementation of the circular economy business models specifically 
identified for the Lombardy Region and based on A2C our solution while provides relevant territorial inputs to project 
other tasks (those within T7.5). Moreover, they will be involved in the Validation of the A2C multidimensional 
model through cross-fertilization (T7.7).  
TCA will actively participate in Task T7.1 analyse within their region successful community -based innovation 
schemes in order to further deliver within the A2C systemic model. 
They will also participate in T8.6 on knowledge transfer activities and in WP8 as a whole , providing a valuable 
contribution to A2C Outreach impact through their wide networks.  
 
Related experience: TCA has an extensive knowledge in the agrifood sector since they work closely with several 
agrifood companies  of different sectors as fruits & vegetables, meat,  cured-meat, dairy, bakery, farinaceous, 
beverages and they integrate horizontally and vertically the main industrial players of the agrifood chain. TCA, 

Section 4,5,6-page 88 
 
can also bring all its Network and connections in the agrifood sector such as Italian agrifood clusters at national level 
(CLAN) and the Lombardy High Tech Cluster for Agrofood (CAT.AL) 
 
INTEREST IN THE PROJECT 
 
Participation in A2C project will allow TCA increasing knowledge in innovative circular business models and 
introducing them to their clients. Moreover, they will promote the replication to show the innovation in the sector 
and will create new lines for training in the agrifood sector.

4.1.41 LPK -LIETUVOS PRAMONININKU KONFEDRACIJA  
 
PARTNER Nº: 41 (NGO)

SHORTNAME: LPK  
LOCATION: Spain 
ROLE IN THE PROJECT: PARTNER. 
 
DESCRIPTION OF THE LEGAL ENTITY  
 
The Lithuanian Confederation of Industrialists (LPK) is a major business association in Lithuania which represents 
the interests of industrialists and employers. LPK is an independent, non -political organization that represents public 
interests of all busi ness groups in Lithuania. LPK unites  50 trade and seven regional business associations with  over 
3000 major business and manufacturing enterprises of the country, as well as 26 largest non-associated enterprises 
and corporations.  LPK members include most L ithuanian production enterprises, banks, trading companies, 
representative offices of foreign firms, research institutes, and educational establishments. 
 
The Confederation in an umbrella organization uniting 51 branch and 5 regional associations  which comprise 
over 3000 medium and large enterprises from various public and private sectors.  
Main objectives of the LPK are:  
 To create the most favourable conditions for economic, technical and social progress of Lithuanian 
enterprises regardless their ownership form,  
 To represent rights of the members of LPK and to defend their interests in the governmental, social and 
international structures, to expand markets for products and raw materials, to improve conditions of exports 
as well as for imports in order to  enable Lithuanian industry to contribute a proper contribution to the 
strengthening of the national economy,  
 To represent entrepreneurs and defend their social and legal interests,  
 To make contacts among Lithuanian manufacturers and their counterparts a broad through international 
exhibitions and available information systems.  
The member of LPK acting in Agrifood sector is  Lithuanian Food Exporters Association (LitMEA) which 
unites food industry companies and coordinates SMART FOOD cluster and Digital I nnovation Hub 
‘AgriFood Lithuania’ which brings together major research, business and public stakeholders in Lithuania for the 
common mission of transforming agriculture, food and associated sectors with digital-based innovations.  
 
The DIH in Lithuania focused exclusively on agriculture and food, the DIH is extensively working with and 
promoting the application of breakthrough technologies in agri -food, with experience in key technological areas of 
Artificial Intelligence, remote sensing, Internet of Things, Robotics, distributed IT systems, and other digital -based 
technologies. The DIH links its stakeholders with international and cross-sector initiatives, provides all-round support 
in the research, development and deployment of agri-food innovations, and has been acknowledged by the EC Joint 
Research Centre as an example DIH with excellence in sectorial specialization.   
AgriFood Lithuania participates in international R&D&I projects (such as SmartAgriFood, Robotics4EU, CoRoSect, 
FLEXIGROBOTS) and together with a consortium of partners under the EDIH4IAE.LT initiative has been selected 
as Lithuania’s first proposed choice for becoming a European Digital Innovation Hub . The DIH organizes annual 
international events, such as the ‘AgriFood Forum’ (www.digitalfarm.lt) for business and policy leaders, and ‘HACK 
AgriFood’ (www.hackagrifood.lt) – the first annual agri-food technology hackathon in Lithuania.

RELEVANT PUBLICATIONS, PRODUCTS, SERVICES, OR OTHER ACHIEVEMENTS 
 Innovation support services . AgriFood Lithuanian DIH is a one -stop-shop for agriculture and food industry 
innovations, and provides its members and stakeholders with all -rounded services supporting innovation 
development, demonstration and commercialization. This includes: (1) Collaboration, stakeholder ecosystem 
building and synergy creation; (2) Facilitating international technology, competence and know-how transfer; (3) 
Innovation co-creation, R&D project development support and funding accessibility; (4) Solution prototyping,

commercialization and investment readiness services; (6) Mentoring, training and experience sharing. 
 International innovation events . AgriFood Lithuania DIH has expertise in organising international events

at innovation consulting for local farmers. The DIH is co-organizing annual events, such as the ‘AgriFood Forum’ 
(www.digitalfarm.lt, international conference for agri -food sector experts, policy makers and business leaders) 
and ‘HACK AgriFood’ ( www.hackagrifood.lt, the first annual hackathon in Lithuania focused exclusively on 
agriculture and food technologies). 
 Outreach to stakeholders and competence networks. AgriFood Lithuania DIH is a member of and participant 
within cross-European competence networks (SmartAgriHubs, EIT Food) and national (Smart Food Cluster, 
Lithuanian Farmers Union Lithuanian Farmers’ Union, Chamber of Agriculture, National Paying Agency) 
business and public stakeholders. 
RELEVANT PROJECTS OR ACTIVITIES 
 ERASMUS+ (2018-2020): CIM “Promoting Creativity and Innovation Management in an innovative blended 
learning and validation programme at the interface between higher education (HE) and business” (CIM) 
 “Partners 4 Value”  initiative is the Consortium established between the Lithuanian Confederation of 
Industrialists (LPK) and the leading Lithuanian universities The Initiative  is established at national level in 
Lithuania aiming to expand “real life” training opportunities for students and university personnel through 
Business-International Organisations-University partnerships. 
 GLL Business Advisors  program is connecting international Lithuanian experts passionate about helping 
Lithuanian businesses to get ahead. The program brings together Lithuania’s most successful and experienced 
international professionals to advice to Lithuania’s growing companies, emerging innovators and entrepreneurs.  
 SmartAgriHubs: Connecting the dots to unleash the innovation potential for digital transformation of 
the European agri-food sector (2018-2022) – project under the H2020 framework (H2020-EU.3.2.1.3., grant 
no. 818182) with a consortium of 105 initial international partners, coordinated by Wageningen University & 
Research (Netherlands). SmartAgriHubs is dedicated to accelerate the digital transformation of the European 
agri-food sector. It will consolidate, activate and extend the current ecosystem by building a network of Digital 
Innovation Hubs (DIHs) that will boost the uptake of digital solutions by the farming sector. This will be 
Section 4,5,6-page 91 
 
achieved by integrating technology and business support in a local one-stop-shop approach involving all 
regions and all relevant players in Europe. The project is formed by 28 flagship innovation experiments 
demonstrating digital innovations in agriculture, facilitated by DIHs from 9 Regional Clusters including all 
European member states. SmartAgriHubs will improve the maturity of innovation services of DIHs so that 
digital innovations will be replicated across Europe and widely adopted by European farmers. 
(https://smartagrihubs.eu) 
 FLEXIGROBOTS: Flexible robots for intelligent automation of precision agriculture operations (2021-
2024) – project under the H2020 framework (H2020-EU.2.1.1., grant no. 101017111) with a consortium of 16 
international partners, coordinated by Atos (Spain). FlexiGroBots proposes a Platform for developing 
heterogeneous multi-robot systems and applications which allows for i) more versatility by using the same 
robots for different observation and intervention tasks, in different missions, throughout the crop life cycle, ii) 
more cooperation between heterogeneous (ground and aerial) robots to accomplish more complex missions; iii) 
more valuable data to generate accurate insights into the fields, crops and robotics operations by combining 
data from IoT sensors, satellites and data collected by the robots; iv) more autonomy for real-time adaptation of 
mission plans as well as robot behaviour at the crop level, given operational conditions and real-time insights; 
v) more precision to carry out specific tasks in a very localized way, gaining accuracy and lowering costs. 
 Development of the Future Technologies Digital Innovation Hub (2021-2024) – joint major national 
investment project (€10,7M budget with €6M in public funding) by Visoriai Information Technology Park and 
AgriFood Lithuania DIH for the development of digital innovation hub infrastructure and its innovation 
support services. The project will provide funding to support start-ups, SMEs and innovation end-users with 
test-before-invest demonstration, novel solution validation, adaptation, market innovation and similar support 
services based on the de minimis model within the upcoming 3-year period.

A Stakeholders Panel will be established during A2C implementation representing the main audience and sectors 
of the A2C (agrifood sectors, plastic recycling, packaging, agriculture, biotechnology, bioeconomy & circular

The Stakeholders Panel will support the Consortium during the project developments with recommendations and will 
be involved in the communication actions. The board will be formed by experts from these disciplines as is it shown 
in the following table.  
Currently, we have the letter of adhesion to our Stakeholders Panel of the Unione Nazionale Consumatori Umbria 
(see letter of adhesion attached in Section 6). Unione Nazionale Consumatori Umbria is a branch of Unione Nazionale 
Consumatori (UNC) and it is the first consumer association in Italy . Its foundation dates back to 1955. UNC is 
independent from political parties and trade unions and is a social promotion association. Its exclusive purpose and 
main objective is the protection and representation of consumers , pursued through the proposal of new 
regulations, assistance, information, education and orientation activities aimed at citizens through its branches. 
At the same time, UNC is an important channel of communication thanks to its National Consumer Council 
membership at the Ministry of the Economy, sponsored by the Ministry of Social Affairs and Labour. It is a social 
promotion association approved by the M inistry of Labour and Social Policies  and it is registered in the list of the 
Umbria Region ex l.r.17/2014, Transparency Register EU No 734603432102-03. 
Furthermore, European Commission recognize UNC as a consumer association in Italy. UNC is a member of 
Consumers' International, of Consumers' Forum and of the Centre for Juridical Studies on Consumer Rights. 
Its activities also have the recent patronage of the Senate of the Italian Republic. 
UNC collaborate with public authorities such as the Region of Umbria, the Province of Perugia, the Chamber of 
Commerce, Industry, Crafts and Agriculture, educational institutions and various Umbrian municipalities and we 
have organized numerous training conferences and activities for the territory 
Main activities of  UNC include: 
▪ Protecting the health, economic and legal interests of consumers and users, as well as their right to adequate 
information and proper advertising 
▪ Promoting the development of a more informed and influential relationship with the manufacturers, distributors 
and services providers 
▪ Promoting critical, responsible and conscious consumer education and informing and educating consumers with 
every possible means and with appropriate information, advice and assistance services 
▪ Controlling and improving the safety and quality of products and services, including actions to encourage new 
traceability and quality systems 
▪ Participating in the sustainable development of the regional economy and society, in particular in the meanings of 
corporate social responsibility 
▪ Promoting the dissemination of consumption practices oriented towards respect for environmental and ethical 
values 
▪ Promoting events and initiatives such as conferences, meetings, seminars and courses, including school and 
university about information, education and orientation and using journalistic space, radio and television to raise 
consumer awareness on the importance and influence of their more rational and more supportive behaviour, being 
informed of the prices and the quality o f the products and services available on the market, in order to do not to 
be victims of abuse, speculation and fraud;

AGRO2CIRCULAR and the national legal and ethics requirements of countries and institutions involved.   
The present section reviews ethical issues attached to both  research in this project  and the development of 
TECHNOLOGIES FOR PLASTIC AND FOOD WASTE. Ethics is an integral part of research that will be 
considered from the beginning to the end of the project implementation, in particular, because of the participation of 
workers, some of them low-skilled. The system will be implemented in environment such that workers will be 
involved; circular economy and agri-food sector comprise both together high-skilled and low-skilled workers. 
The last group is considered vulnerable due to their psycho -social risks, the credentials obtained, the salary 
income and, even, health conditions and exposition to risk factors. Other ethical issues are also examined in-light 
on the current evidence.   
Section 4,5,6-page 101 
 
In this regard, it should be emphasised that all academic  and/or research entities involved currently have Ethics 
Committees. These committees could follow different protocols and approval procedures , but, at first instance, the 
research entity must justify all issues considering the informed consent and data privacy/anonymization of the 
information collected exposing why the research to be carried out is necessary, relevant and appropriate. Then, the  
Ethics Committee could proceed to approve, amend, or reject the research activity.  
Companies and for -profit entities, and also those without an internal Ethical Committee, should guide their 
procedures in line with the statements and agreements reached during the Ethics Management process, as well as the 
Ethics framework, drafted along this section 5 and formally delivered at the beginning of the project.  
Thus, all the activities developed along the project will comply with ethical principles and relevant national, EU and 
international legislation, for example, the  Chapter of Fundamental Rights of the EU and the European Convention 
on Human Rights.  
Provisions of Directive 95/46/EC and the General Data Protection Regulation (proposed in (European Commission, 
2012)) are shown to be highly relevant to the protection of research participants and service users.   
The General Data Protection Regulation  
Observing existing European regulation already provides many safeguards ensuring that data privacy  is 
maintained throughout any research or service delivery process. By transposing Directive 95/46/EC, Member States 
jointly set legally binding rules for the protection of individuals  regarding the processing of personal data. The 
European Commission recently drafte d a Regulation (European Commission, 2012) with the intention of 
comprehensive reform of EU data protection rules. The following assessment is primarily based on the Directive 
currently in force (European Parliament, 1995), but is expected to remain substa ntially unchanged under the 
Regulation.  
The notion of data subjects' consent to processing is to be harmonised and the criterion added consent be "explicit". 
The data will be accessible only for the participants involved in the project and no transfer to any third party will be 
made. During the project, the project board must make a decision on the date of destruction. A common trend in this 
area is to destroy them after 10 years from the project end date.  
Data controllers will be required to implement co nformant policies and processes (Art. 22) including notifying any 
breach without delay. Data subjects' rights to have data deleted - "right to be forgotten" (Art. 17) - and to receive free 
copies of their data, are to be strengthened. Privacy impact assess ments will be required if processing is deemed 
risky, and all but the smallest companies must appoint a data protection officer and notify details to their national 
authority.   
These provisions will effectively implement an intended ‘privacy by design’ (A rt. 23), though many details remain 
to be finalised. The draft Regulation gives the Commission powers  to extend the rules subsequently  by means 
of delegated and implementing acts.   
When approved, the Regulation would apply from 2015 on, uniformly across t he EU, unlike the current Directive 
which has seen variable transposition into national legislation. It  is therefore intended that the project conforms to 
the requirements of the Regulation in research processes and in service design addition to national legislation in force 
based on the Directive.   
The project coordinator (CETEC) commits to following the relevant national legislation supported by all partners - 
and later EU regulation - where it  applies to  the work carried out. All measures necessary to i mplement the 
appropriate legislation will be taken. At an early stage in the project, a review of national legislation and relevant 
good practices will be carried out to enable the elaboration of a comprehensive data protection management roadmap 
for the entire project duration. The roadmap is:   
1. to provide a detailed specification of how these are to be met in terms of processes, tools, 
responsibilities and timing and   
2. to contain a clear assignment of tasks and responsibility to the persons that need to be involved.   
3. To monitor and follow up the implementation of adequate cyber-security measures  
It is concluded that observation of existing safeguards and procedures will allow the project to complete its 
programme of research in an ethically acceptable way and enable development of services which in their turn can be 
expected to establish Best Practice in meeting current ethical concerns attached to collect or use any personal data 
where privacy concerns can be paramount.   
As well as conforming to law both in force and in preparation, a well-accepted approach to ethics in service provision 
will be adopted, upholding the central principles of autonomy, beneficence, non -malfeasance and justice in all 
operations and as built into service design.  
 
Addressing Ethics issues and Challenges  
In particular, various  ethical issues raised from the research and innovation challenge proposed 
by AGRO2CIRCULAR. These are furtherly explored below.  
  
Research objectives   
Section 4,5,6-page 102 
 
The data collection aims at:   
• Determining the means and ways in which the public engagement can be enhanced 
• researching into current techniques and procedures  
• improving them on the basis of an evidence-based research, so, determining which protocols will 
be cost-effective, even optimal    
• research into the accomplishment of all objectives and expected impact outcomes 
Research methodology  
Ethical issues arise within the project in three respects , namely concerning data privacy, informed consent of 
participants and ensuring ethical compliance for the solution design. In concrete:  
• Data privacy issues will arise in the context of observation of interactions and activities in the job 
place but also in workshops and focus groups, as well as other events for raising the public 
engagement  
• Informed consent will be provided for the participation of individuals directly and indirectly involved 
during the pilot phase (so, directors, mangers and workers, also administrative staff and providers visiting 
the job place if any)    
• The Ethical Solution Design must address the most important risks linked to the  aforementioned 
research and training activities.  
The following sections discuss these points in more detail. Based on an initial review of the regulative situation, it 
will be ensuring that ethics follow not only international but also national regulations.   
A key aspect of any research involving human beings - beyond the researcher(s) actually conducting the research - 
is to protect the interests of participants. When it comes to the proposed research p lan, individuals will be involved 
in the public engagement strategy and other relevant actions (see Section 1 on Excellence). As it has been explained 
above, these individuals could be directors, explicitly consenting the participation of the whole staff in the pilot phase, 
managers and workers, some of them low -skilled and, consequently, particularly considered attending to their 
vulnerability. The implementation of AGRO2CIRCULAR requires the intervention and participation of 
workers, some of them being po tentially low-skilled and/or living under vulnerable conditions. Thus, in this 
case is crucial and essential to assure the confidentiality of data, the proportionate use of those data 
obtained and also the minimal risk of psycho-social or physical harm to all participants. The European Union 
has stipulated that all research activities are to be carried out in compliance with fundamental ethical 
principles according to Section 4 of the Administrative form (part A).   
In addition, any ethical requirement, inc luding randomisation process will follow the protocols agreed by the 
international research community (see section below).    
 
Data privacy  
The basic principles for processing personal data centre on transparency, general prohibition, legitimate purpose and 
proportionality:  
Transparency: The data subject has the right to be informed when his/her personal data are  being processed. The 
data controller must provide his/her name and address, the purpose of processing, the recipients of the data and all 
other information required to ensure the processing is fair (Art. 10). Data subjects – or their legal representatives - 
have the right to access all personal data referring to them and to demand the rectification, deletion or blocking of 
data that is incomplete, inaccurate or not being processed in compliance with the Directive (Art. 12).  
General prohibition: Personal data may not be processed unless one of the following circumstances apply (Art. 7):   
• the data subject has consented to the processing;  
• processing is necessary: for the performance of or the entering into a contract, for compliance with a legal 
obligation, to protect the vital interests of the data subject or for the performance of a task carried out in the 
public interest or in the exercise of official authority;  
• processing is necessary for the purposes of the legitimate interests pursued by the controller or by the third 
party or parties to whom the data are disclosed, unless overridden by fundamental rights and freedoms of the data 
subject  
While to process personal data referring to religious beliefs, political opinions, health, sexual orientation or race are 
neither required nor scoped within the project, this information could be inferred from some surveys and focus groups 
within the Social Impact assessment tasks. It is being stated a general prohibition to collect this information (Art. 8). 
To process religious or political data without the explicit consent must require additional safeguards; as a 
consequence, it will be avoided.   
Legitimate purpose: Personal data can only be processed for specified explicit and legitimate purposes and may not 
be processed further in a way incompatible with those purposes. The  Member States  are required to lay down 
appropriate safeguards for personal data stored for longer periods for historical, statistical or scientific use. (Art. 6)   
Proportionality: Personal data may be processed only insofar as it is adequate, relevant and not excessive about the 
purposes for which they are coll ected and further processed. The data must be accurate and, where necessary, kept 
Section 4,5,6-page 103 
 
up to date; every reasonable step must be taken to ensure that data which are inaccurate or incomplete, having regard 
to the purposes for which they  were collected or for which they are further processed, are erased or rectified. The 
data should not be kept in a form which permits identification of data subjects (that is, as  personal data) for longer 
than is necessary for the purposes for which the data have been collected or processed.   
 
As is the nature of the minimum harmonisation of the Directive, national regulation may add to the principles set out 
there, and their jurisdictions may develop particular interpretations of the stipulations in the Directive, after 
translation and transposition. It will take into account throughout the project’s lifecycle. A data protection framework 
will be developed and applied as part of a dedicated data protection management task. Also, the data protection 
management task in the work plan helps ensure that the project will comply with any national regulation relevant to 
the project in the validation site countries involved.   
 
Hands-on guidance on how to ensure data privacy throughout the project’s lifecycle research will  be derived from 
this. An initial set of rules to be followed has been derived at this stage already and will  be consolidated during the 
start-up phase of the project:  
• Only personnel within the participating organisations will have access to non -exploited and unprocessed 
data. Then, these data should be anonymized if applicable.  
• Only aggregate summaries of data will be available to external parties.   
• Participants will  be voluntarily involved in the project and  treated with respect at all  times, and their 
anonymity will be protected. They will be able to abandon the project at any time.  
• All data will be kept in anonymous form wherever possible.  pseudo-anonymization though codes will be 
used to replace any conventional identifiers within the data where full  anonymization is not possible for the 
purposes declared and followed: By making data anonymous, the privacy of individuals will be maintained. The 
essential point is that the  AGRO2CIRCULAR partners analysing the data will not be in a position to associate 
the data with an identifiable individual. The pseudo-anonymization process starts once a participant allows the 
use of data in the study. At this moment,  AGRO2CIRCULAR will designate a responsible to carry out this 
process. First of all, he/she will create a  database (with restricted access, only available with his/her password) 
in which he/she will add files containing the reference of the data that is going to be used. These references and 
the file name will be a random code obtained using certified and secured pseudo-anonymization software.   
• The data security supervisor designated in each centre will verify that files have been codified properly and 
that they accomplish with privacy rights; at this time, files can be transferred to the rest of the partners.  
• Organisations holding the data are the guardians and should exercise due diligence of its protection.    
 
Implementation and personal data. Personal data - e.g. from transcribed interviews, if any, conducted during the 
implementation and during the WP7 and the public engagement activities will be stored securely at all times and 
deleted as soon as the purpose of their collection has  been fulfilled. Storage and destruction of data storage media 
will be documented. A list of all researchers given access to any per sonal data will  be set  up and 
maintained. Quotations from interviews will  be included  in reports and publications arising from the research. 
Quotations will be anonymised using e.g. a pseudonym. If participants wish to talk to interviewers about sensitive 
issues which they wish to remain confidential, interviewers will not use what they hear in this context in any part of 
the research.   
 
 
Informed explicit consent  
Explicit informed consent by data subjects is a vital part of any process involving human subjects, to respect their 
rights of free choice and to data privacy. The whole consortium will ensure that the potential subjects can reach a 
truly informed decision about whether or not to participate in the research.  Their explicit informed consent must be 
given freely, without coercion, and must  be based  on a clear  understanding of what participation involves, 
including any personal data to be processed and the purposes of the same. Each partner responsible for involving 
end-users will guarantee the coll ection of explicit consents, signed by representatives as stated above before any 
empirical activity affecting them, including but not limited to the collecting of personal data. As in the case of data 
protection, consolidated hands-on guidance in this res pect will be collected from relevant sources at an early stage 
of the project. A preliminary investigation  has revealed some principles that should be considered about achieving 
informed consent from users participating in the project:  
• Each potential subject must be adequately informed of the aims, methods, anticipated benefits and potential 
hazards of the research and any discomfort it may entail;  
• The expected participation of individuals and the approximate duration of each activity.  
• Any documentation given to potential participants should be  comprehensible, and there should be an 
opportunity for them to raise any issues of concern;  
Section 4,5,6-page 104 
 
• Consent should  be given  in writing and records of consent should be maintained; considering the 
particularities in the workplace and the risks that it may entail, the following measures may be also considered:  
o Workers’ representatives consent: If to obtain individual consent from each worker is not feasible, 
labour unions representatives will discuss and agreed on the parti cipation with the staff and, then, sign 
in the consent.    
o Consent Form: The information in the consent form should be  individualised and communicated in 
a way that is more sensitive to needs of participants. The number of questions in the “Consent Form” has 
to be appropriate to the participants’ profile (high -skilled workers and managers, chiefs and directors, 
low-skilled workers, labor union officers, etc.)  
• Potential participants must be informed that they are free to withdraw consent to participation at any time;  
• There should be a procedure for making complaints and participants should be made aware of this;  
• All participants should be volunteers. Considerable care should be taken where consent is sought from those 
in a dependent position.     
• Any inducement offered to participants should be declared and should be by appropriate guidelines;  
Potential impact of the research    
Beyond the issues of data privacy, a broader range of ethics issues potentially arise when designing and implementing 
new services for citizens. One aspect is the  input collected from persons’ data, such as movements, procedures in 
the job place, customs, etc. and the other one is the interaction of the project with the daily routines and processes 
in the job place.    
Pseudo-anonymisation of data  
As regards the Data Collection, and beyond the issues of data privacy, a broader range of ethics issues 
potentially arises. One aspect is the input collected from the entities taking part in the pilot phase and, specifically, 
workers involved.      
AGRO2CIRCULAR obtains and deliver data about human subjected from a vast range of activities, mainly the 
implementation of the system and the public engagement activities; all differentiated legal frameworks as regards the 
protection of workers, although issues related to data privacy, are harmonised under the RGPD.   
Exploitation of data  
The exploitation of data refers to the re-use of massive data, instead of personal data. However, the use of these data 
(secondary use) is regulated within the Digital Single Market and also regulated by directives related to consumers’ 
rights, personal data and the GDPR, among others.    It is expected to facilitate the exchange of data between agri -
food players. However, it depends on the regulations for the Public Sector Information (PSI) within the Digital Single 
Market; currently, a group on Data Policy and Innovation works along the data -value chain to provide a legal and 
political framework that allows public authorities, companies and citizens to make the most of data in all sectors of 
the economy and many aspects of society. Results from the consultation 1 and new legal frameworks and directives 
will be revised by the consortium as the project progresses-   
The European Commission announced the foundation of a Support Centre for Data Sharing between research and 
private sector data, under the Connecting Europe Facility. This Support Centre will provide tools and guidelines that 
should be followed during the AGRI2CIRCULAR project.    
Ethical risks and AGRO2CIRCULAR potential  
The field of innovation and research often finds difficult to assign a specific code; the vast majority of them are based 
on the  teleological approach (utilitarian and consequentialism) or the  deontological perspective. The Ethics Code 
aims at determining the proper courses of action and indicate which ones should be followed. The Ethics and the 
Data Protection strategy are being differentiated within this project, if the Ethics Code must be consistent with the 
European directives but its implication are wide and should be justified for a wide range of individuals independently 
of their cultural background and environment.   Ethics are also important in this area because of the power that 
researchers and developers working on Data Sciences and  monitoring tools have to affect the social and physical 
environment.   
From a practical point of view, the principle -based approach to Ethics allows for the incorporation of elements of 
ethical theories through the formulation of core principles.  These core principles must be considered to design and 
implement the co-creation, as well as during the whole Pilot Phase. Similar perspectives could be found in the work 
performed by ethics committees in the bioethics field and has also been adopted by many professional guidelines 
and codes of conduct,  as well as in more general formulations of guiding principles; for instance, the  European 
Group on Ethics in Science and New Technologies (EGE)   
In the case of AGRO2CIRCULAR, these principles are being accepted by the consortium:  
• Autonomy: the idea behind the principle of autonomy is that the decisions and choices of grown -up 
persons need to be respected and not interfered with (within the limits of one’s autonomy, harm to ot her 
persons is not tolerated);   
• Beneficence: the idea behind the principle of beneficence is that we ought to do other persons good;  
• Non-malfeasance: means avoiding doing harm and harming other persons with our actions;  
Section 4,5,6-page 105 
 
• Justice: justice is about the distribution of benefits and burdens and finding a just way of doing so. 
Often justice is simply referred to regarding fairness. Depending on our interpretation of justice, different 
solutions to redistribution of scarce resources can be found. Examples inclu de distribution according to 
need and merit.  
These principles, together with utility, intrinsic value, equal consideration of interests and consequences over time 
will guide the procedures and the Ethics framework, ensuring an evidence-based and robust framework.  
Main risks related to innovation and research activities, specifically in the AGRO2CIRCULAR project, are:  
Ethical 
risk 
No.

ER1  The system gathers data from the

The Principle of Explicability  
A vast amount of information about workers and their routines, customs and habits will 
be gathered and some of  this information is out of the scope of the data gathering and 
refers to personal issues.  
This information supposes a major risk, as long as it could be used against the worker 
(e.g. mobbing). At the same time, if the worker is aware about this observation and data 
gathering, it may reduce his/her autonomy in the job plac e and might interfere with 
private issues which do not affect the performance and productivity.   
It is necessary to get approval by the representatives (e .g., labour unions if any) and to 
guarantee that the data regarding workers will be treated in a fair and adequate manner.   
Specific measures will be implemented and delivered to the EC when elaborating the 
Ethics protocol in order to ensure that the information obtained and non-relevant for the 
assessment of the performance in the job place will not be used against the persons 
involved.  
ER2  The project could only show an 
instrumental value, neither intrinsic nor 
societal value  
Principle of Intrinsic value  – Innovation and research must be intrinsically valuable, 
going far beyond their  instrumental value. AGRI2CIRCULAR goes beyond the state 
of the art providing new tools for preventing food and plastic waste and for enhancing 
circularity and circular economy innovative business models, showing a long -term 
impact on the environment and, also, the sustainability of jobs.   
ER3  AGRO2CIRCULAR potential for 
improving current wellbeing and life 
conditions of end-users is limited  
Principle of Utility  – Innovation and research must improve the life and wellbeing of 
their users, fulfilling the needs of the clear majority of people  and, specifically, their 
end-users at long-term. At least, Data Collection, analysis and open data/sharing must 
have the potential for improving the  current life conditions  for as much people as 
possible, avoiding thos e interventions that could  undermine their  well-being and 
produce a foreseeable damage.  All unforeseen risks must be monitored and mitigated, 
even avoided. From the point of view of the whole society, the impact on the 
environment is quite clear; however,  the impact on workers and the potential of these 
systems for improving their lives and job conditions at medium and long -term is 
particularly challenging. This risk is linked and considering jointly with the next one:  
ER4 AGRO2CIRCULAR partnership is no t 
considering including all potentially 
affected interests  
Principle of equal consideration of interests  – While companies and research entities 
can follow their legitimate purposes as regards efficiency and business growth 
respectively, the  AGRI2CIRCULAR consortium should both  include all affected 
interests when calculating the rightness of a certain innovation or research activity. In 
this case,  the public engagement strategy and activities in WP7 will integrate the 
public’s views and voices within the project lifecycle.   
ER5 AGRO2CIRCULARmust show posit ive 
consequences and changes at long-term  
Principle of consequences over time : The research and innovation impact must be 
assessed at long-term for evaluating its Ethical adequacy in-light of the considerations 
previously detailed.  
   
The gender and ethnicity must be taken into consideration overcoming all potential barriers detected with this regard 
in order to raise the positive impacts over the vast majority of the individuals.
